Richard Leakey and Roger Lewin, [3mOrigins Reconsidered [0m(New York: Doubleday, 1992). Leakey, who is best known for his discoveries of 
fossils much older than those relevant to the above discussion, is nonetheless insightful and provocative on the Neandertal 
question. He started off believing in regional continuity, partly because he found appealing the apparent inevitability of the 
emergence of modern humans from all sorts of archaic peoples around the globe. But he gradually came to lean toward โout of 
Africa,โ with its even more appealing implication that all the races of todayโs world are one people.

controversies. Trinkaus, an anthropologist, is one of the leading proponents of regional continuity, but his historiography is 
admirably unbiased. The bookโs only flaw is the authorsโ tendency to interject thumbnail sketches of the leading scientists, whose 
livesโat least as described hereโwere not, in general, as interesting as their discoveries or ideas.

Geoffrey of Monmouth, [3mThe History of the Kings of Britain, [0mtrans. Lewis Thorpe (London: The Folio Society, 1966). Just as it was 
when Geoffrey finished it in 1138, the [3mHistory [0mis still entertaining, intriguing โฆ and ultimately unreliable. 

Gerald Hawkins, [3mStonehenge Decoded [0m(Garden City, N.Y.: Doubleday, 1965). In spite of his flaws, Hawkins had a flair for drama, and 
the book still makes for exciting reading. 

Richard Atkinson, โMoonshine on Stonehenge,โ [3mAntiquity [0m40, no. 159 (September 1966). The leading archaeologistโs response to 
Hawkins. 

Jacquetta Hawkes, โGod in the Machine,โ [3mAntiquity [0m41, no. 163 (September 1967). Hawkes is rightly famous for saying that โevery 
age gets the Stonehenge it desires, or deserves.โ Her words could just as appropriately be applied to just about every mystery of 
history in this book. 

Christopher Chippindale, [3mStonehenge Complete [0m(Ithaca, N.Y.: Cornell University Press, 1983). Though โcompleteโ can only be an 
overstatement when the subject is Stonehenge, the book is a very thorough historiography that includes just about โeverything 
important, interesting, or odd that has been written or painted, discovered or felt, about the most extraordinary of all ancient 
buildings.โ 

Rodney Castleden, [3mThe Making of Stonehenge [0m(London: Routledge, 1993). A close look at each phase in the monumentโs rise and fall. 

R. M. Cleal, K. Walker, and R. Montague, [3mStonehenge in Its Landscape [0m(London: English Heritage, 1995). A fat technical report that 
brings together all of the results of all of the twentieth centuryโs crucial excavations. 

John North, [3mStonehenge [0m(New York: The Free Press, 1996). The latest and most thorough presentation of the astronomical thesis. 
Northโs thesis, which impressed many but convinced fewer, is that Stonehenge embodied many significant alignments, but that 
previous astronomers have failed to recognize them since they looked at the sun from the center of the monument, when they should 
have been doing so from outside the circle. 

David Souden, [3mStonehenge [0m(London: Collins & Brown, 1997). Commissioned by English Heritage, the quasi-independent agency that 
controls the monument, this is a clear exposition of the orthodox position, accepting some (but rejecting most) astronomical 
theories. 

Barry Cunliffe and Colin Renfrew, eds., [3mScience and Stonehenge [0m(Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1997). A collection of essays 
that grew out of a conference held after the appearance of [3mStonehenge in Its Landscape [0m; includes the latest entries in the 
bluestone and astronomy debates.

Mark Lehner, [3mThe Complete Pyramids [0m(London: Thames & Hudson, 1997). Pyramid by pyramid, everything you wanted to know about each.

[1mููุฒูุฏ ูู ุงูุจุญุซ [0m

Arthur Evans, [3mThe Palace of Minos [0m(London: Macmillan, 1921โ1936). Evansโs own account of the discoveries at Knossos, in four 
volumes. 

Anne Ward, ed., [3mThe Quest for Theseus [0m(New York: Praeger, 1970). Essays on how the Theseus legend originated and developed in art 
and literature, from the classical era to the present. 

Hans Wunderlich, [3mThe Secret of Crete, [0mtrans. from German by Richard Winston (New York: Macmillan, 1974). Wunderlich argues, 
provocatively but ultimately unconvincingly, that Knossos was never lived in, but was, like the Egyptian pyramids, a royal tomb. 

Sylvia Horwitz, [3mThe Find of a Lifetime [0m(New York: Viking, 1981). A readable biography of Evans, with a balanced presentation of 
the controversies until its publication. 

D. A. Hardy et al., [3mThera and the Aegean World III [0m(London: Thera Foundation, 1990). These proceedings of a major international 
conference, held in Thera in 1989, include more than a hundred papers by archaeologists and other scientists. The clear consensus 
was that the Theran eruption was [3mnot [0mresponsible for the end of Minoan civilization. 

J. Lesley Fitton, [3mThe Discovery of the Greek Bronze Age [0m(Cambridge, Mass.: Harvard, University Press, 1996). An authoritative 
account of the excavations at Troy, Mycenae, Knossos, Thera, and other Greek Bronze Age sites. 

Rodney Castleden, [3mAtlantis Destroyed [0m(New York: Routledge, 1998). Other Greek legends besides Theseusโs may be rooted in Minoan 
Crete. Of these, the best known is undoubtedly that of Atlantis, which Plato (writing in the fifth century B.C.) described as a 
great island civilization that, following earthquakes and floods, was swallowed up by the sea. Many writers have speculated that 
Crete was Atlantis, destroyed by the Thera volcano and accompanying earthquakes and floods. This is an argument that faces many 
obstacles, and not just those cited in this chapter. For example, Plato described Atlantis as having been in the Atlantic, though 
Crete is clearly in the Mediterranean. And Plato put the destruction nine thousand years before his time, while the actual span 
was closer to nine hundred years. Castleden is the latest to make the case for Crete as Atlantis, and heโs also one of the most 
reasonable, but it remains an unlikely scenario. Sometimes myths are just myths.

Heinrich Schliemann, [3mTroy and Its Remains [0m(London: John Murray, 1875). Schliemannโs own account of his 1871โ1873 excavation, 
including the discovery of Priamโs treasure. 

Carl Blegen, [3mTroy and the Trojans [0m(New York: Praeger, 1963). Blegenโs version, based on his 1932โ1938 excavations. 

John M. Cook, [3mThe Troad [0m(Oxford: The Clarendon Press, 1973). A study of the archaeology in and around Hisarlik, including a 
comprehensive survey of pre-Schliemann theories. Many of these theories located Troy near the Turkish village of Pinarbasi, and 
Schliemann himself dug there before turning to Hisarlik. 

Michael Wood, [3mIn Search of the Trojan War [0m(New York: Facts On File, 1985). A companion to a BBC program, this provides a good 
introduction to Troy historiography, along with an intriguing and provocative look at the Hittite evidence. 

William Calder III and David Traill, [3mMyth, Scandal, and History [0m(Detroit: Wayne State University Press, 1986). A collection of 
essays portraying Schliemann as a pathological liar, a thesis more fully developed in Traillโs 1995 biography. 

David Traill, [3mSchliemann of Troy [0m(New York: St. Martinโs Press, 1995). An extremely controversial, all-out attack on Schliemann, 
accusing him ofโamong other thingsโcheating his business partners; lying to gain American citizenship; failing to give credit to 
Frank Calvert; and, most devastating of all, making up the story of how and where he found Priamโs treasure. According to Traill, 
Schliemann lied about hiding the treasure in his wifeโs shawl to conceal the fact that heโd actually gathered the objects in the 
treasure from a variety of places in and around Hisarlik, then bunched them together so he could pretend heโd made a dramatic 
discovery. Traillโs damning evidence includes the indisputable fact that Schliemannโs wife was in Athens at the time of the 
discovery. Traillโs critics argue that it would have been impossible for Schliemann to bring together so many objects, all of 
which were later shown to come from the same period. They also point out that the vast majority of his archaeological notes have 
turned out to be largely accurate. But Traillโs defenders (and other Schliemann detractors) counter that the evidence of 
Schliemannโs lying in his other business and personal dealings is overwhelming. Schliemann, they contend, couldnโt be a Dr. Jekyll 
at Hisarlik while being a Mr. Hyde elsewhere. The book is well worth reading, but donโt lose sight of the fact that the questions 
raised are largely irrelevant to the larger question of what happened at Troy. After all, even Schliemann eventually conceded that 
the part of Troy he first excavated dated back to well before the Trojan War and that Priamโs treasure could not, therefore, have 
belonged to Priam or any of his contemporaries. 

Caroline Moorehead, [3mLost and Found [0m(New York: Viking, 1996). As riveting as the history of Troy is the mysterious fate of Priamโs 
treasure, entertainingly revealed in Mooreheadโs book. Schliemann left the treasure to the German government, which displayed it 
at Berlinโs Museum for Prehistory. In 1945 the treasure disappeared, apparently lost forever. Then, in 1991, a Russian art 
historian and a curator at the Pushkin Museum in Moscow broke the story that the entire treasure was buried in the museumโs 
vaults, having been seized by Soviet troops at the end of World War II. 

Vladimir Tolstikov and Mikhail Treister, [3mThe Gold of Troy, [0mtrans. from the Russian by Christina Sever and Mila Bonnichsen (New 
York: Abrams, 1996). In 1994 the Russians admitted they had the treasure and agreed to make it available to scholars and the 
public. This is the catalog of the first public exhibition in almost fifty years. It includes essays defending Schliemann (and the 
treasure) against Traillโs accusations. 

Susan Allen, [3mFinding the Walls of Troy [0m(Berkeley: University of California Press, 1999). Schliemann, who didnโt want to share the 
spotlight, later minimized Calvertโs contribution; here Allen moves Calvert out of the more famous manโs shadow.

[1mููุฒูุฏ ูู ุงูุจุญุซ [0m

[3mThe New Testament. [0mA best-seller for almost two millennia. While youโre at it, you might want to read the Old Testament, too. 

James Robinson, ed., [3mThe Nag Hammadi Library in English [0m(San Francisco: Harper & Row, 1988). Translations from the Coptic, 
including the Gospel of Thomas. 

David Freidrich Strauss, [3mLife of Jesus Critically Examined [0m(Philadelphia: Fortress Press, 1972). Originally published in German in 
1835 and still a classic of rationalism. 

Albert Schweitzer, [3mThe Quest of the Historical Jesus [0m(New York: Macmillan, 1955). Originally published in German in 1906, 
Schweitzerโs book offered a thorough historiography up to then. Schweitzer devoted his life to practicing medicine on the 
disease-beset coast of Africa, convincing many that he had much in common with the subject of his book. 

Edmund Wilson, [3mThe Dead Sea Scrolls [0m(New York: Oxford University Press, 1969). The 1947 discovery of these documents near the 
shore of the Dead Sea created even more furor than the Nag Hammadi find. Many, including Wilson, believed the texts would change 
our view of Christian origins by shedding light on a sect that had much in common with Jesusโ early followers. Scandalously long 
delays in publishing the scrolls convinced many, again including Wilson, that church leaders were suppressing evidence because it 
challenged the uniqueness of Christianity. In the 1990s, after Wilsonโs death, the scrolls were finally opened to all scholars, 
and most concluded that they confirmed his basic claim that Christianity grew out of first-century Judaism. Theyโve been a 
disappointment, however, to those who hoped to find a direct connection to Jesus. The first part of Wilsonโs book was originally 
published in 1955. 

Hugh Schonfield, [3mThe Passover Plot [0m(Dorset, Eng.: Element, 1965). Paints Jesus as a political revolutionary who deliberately 
provoked the authorities, then arranged to be taken down from the cross alive so he could โriseโ again. 

Elaine Pagels, [3mThe Gnostic Gospels [0m(New York: Random House, 1979). The politics of early Christianity as revealed through the Nag 
Hammadi documents. 

John Dominic Crossan, [3mThe Historical Jesus [0m(San Francisco: HarperCollins, 1991). Jesus as Jewish peasant. Crossan writes clearly 
and passionately, making his later, more pop distillations of this work (of which the most notable is his 1995 book [3mJesus: A 
Revolutionary Biography [0m) largely unnecessary. 

Barbara Thiering, [3mJesus and the Riddle of the Dead Sea Scrolls [0m(San Francisco: HarperCollins, 1992). A dissident scholarโs view of 
the Dead Sea Scrolls as cryptograms about Jesus that reveal that he did not die on the cross but was later revived and traveled 
around the Mediterranean in the company of Peter and Paul. 

A. N. Wilson, [3mJesus: A Life [0m(New York: W. W. Norton, 1992). Wilsonโs theory is that the man who appeared as the resurrected Jesus 
was one of his brothers, probably James, who took the opportunity to seize control over Jesusโ movement. 

Robert Funk, Roy Hoover, and the Jesus Seminar, [3mThe Five Gospels [0m(New York: Macmillan, 1993). When the votes were in, only 18 
percent of the words ascribed to Jesus in the gospels passed as authentic; postpublication votes moved on from his words to his 
deeds and, not surprisingly, the resurrection lost the election. 

E. P. Sanders, [3mThe Historical Figure of Jesus [0m(London: Allen Lane, 1993). Jesus as a Jewish prophet who believed the world was 
about to end. 

John Shelby Spong, [3mResurrection: Myth or Reality? [0m(San Francisco: HarperCollins, 1994). An Episcopal bishopโs intellectual journey 
from a literal to a more symbolic belief in Easter. 

Burton Mack, [3mWho Wrote the New Testament? [0m(San Francisco: HarperCollins, 1995). Mack goes farther than most modern scholars, even 
secular ones, in exposing how the gospels were fictional mythologies only distantly related to the historical Jesus. 

Robert Funk, [3mHonest to Jesus [0m(San Francisco: HarperCollins, 1996). The latest work from the founder of the Jesus Seminar; as 
always, heโs provocative and readable. 

Luke Timothy Johnson, [3mThe Real Jesus [0m(San Francisco: HarperCollins, 1996). A sharp attack on the Jesus Seminar and the โmisguidedโ 
quest for the historical Jesus. 

Russell Shorto, [3mGospel Truth [0m(New York: Riverhead Books, 1997). A journalistโs entertaining survey of the most recent searchers 
for the historical Jesus, in particular the members of the Jesus Seminar. 

Charlotte Allen, [3mThe Human Christ [0m(New York: The Free Press, 1998). A thorough account of the search for the historical Jesus, 
though sometimes colored by Allenโs Catholicism.

Paul Kosok and Maria Reiche, โThe Mysterious Markings of Nazca,โ [3mNatural History [0m(May 1947). The astronomical thesis, with which 
everyone who came after had to (and still has to) contend. 

Erich von Dรคniken, [3mChariots of the Gods? [0m(New York: G. P. Putnamโs Sons, 1969). The Nazca lines make up just one element of von

Gerald Hawkins, [3mBeyond Stonehenge [0m(New York: Harper & Row, 1973). In spite of his negative conclusions about Nazca, Hawkins makes

Tony Morrison, [3mThe Mystery of the Nasca Lines [0m(Suffolk, Eng.: Nonesuch Expeditions, 1987). A fine popular survey, though with a

Evan Hadingham, [3mLines to the Mountain Gods [0m(New York: Random House, 1987). An excellent summary of othersโ theories, leading up to

Anthony Aveni, ed., [3mThe Lines of Nazca [0m(Philadelphia: American Philosophical Society, 1990). A collection of essays by the leading 
researchers, including Clarkson, Urton, Silverman, Ruggles, and Aveni himself, all offering a pan-Andean approach to the lines.

Sylvanus Morley, [3mThe Ancient Maya [0m(Stanford, Calif.: Stanford University Press, 1956; originally published in 1946). A dated but 
impressively thorough survey of Mayan culture. 

J. Eric Thompson, [3mThe Rise and Fall of Maya Civilization [0m(Norman, Okla.: University of Oklahoma Press, 1966; originally published 
in 1954). Many of Thompsonโs ideas have been eclipsed by those of later archaeologists, but the book is still very much worth 
reading. If only Thompsonโs successors had shared his talent for popular writing. 

T. Patrick Culbert, ed., [3mThe Classic Maya Collapse [0m(Albuquerque, N.M.: University of New Mexico Press, 1973). A collection of 
papers from a 1970 conference that was important both in reflecting and in advancing the emerging consensus according to which a 
series of interrelated factors caused the collapse. 

Linda Schele and David Friedel, [3mA Forest of Kings [0m(New York: William Morrow, 1990). Based on their translations of the writings at 
various Mayan centers, Schele and Friedel present the histories of a number of dynasties. The kings emerge as both sophisticated 
and brutal. 

Michael Coe, [3mBreaking the Maya Code [0m(London: Thames & Hudson, 1992). Coe turns the incredibly technical story of the deciphering 
into a narrative thatโs understandable and dramatic, even a bit gossipy. 

Jeremy Sabloff and John Henderson, eds., [3mLowland Maya Civilization in the Eighth Century [0m(Washington, D.C.: Dumbarton Oaks 
Research Library, 1993). A collection of papers from a 1989 conference that included many of the leading thinkers and theories. 

Gene and George Stuart, [3mLost Kingdoms of the Maya [0m(Washington, D.C.: National Geographic Society, 1993). A lavishly illustrated 
view of the Maya by a husband-and-wife team of archaeologists. The Stuartsโ son, David, who first visited Mesoamerica at age 
three, later became a leading scholar of Mayan anthropology. 

David Hodell, Jason Curtis, and Mark Brenner, โPossible Role of Climate in the Collapse of Classic Maya Civilization,โ [3mNature [0m
(June 1995). The case for drought.

John Dos Passos, [3mEaster Island [0m(Garden City, N.Y.: Doubleday, 1971). A useful anthology of excerpts from accounts of the early 
European visitors to the island, including Roggeveen and Cook. Dos Passosโs own visit, which concludes the book, is of much less 
interest. 

Thor Heyerdahl, [3mKon-Tiki [0m(Chicago: Rand McNally, 1950). When it comes to adventures on the sea, Melville has nothing on Heyerdahl. 

Thor Heyerdahl, [3mAku-Aku [0m(Chicago: Rand McNally, 1958). A colorful narrative of the 1955-1956 expedition, no less enjoyable because 
of the authorโs iconoclastic views, though slightly marred by his patronizing attitude toward the islanders. 

Thor Heyerdahl and Edwin Ferdon, Jr., eds., [3mArchaeology of Easter Island [0m(Chicago, Rand McNally, 1961). Reports from Heyerdahlโs 
team, many of whom disagreed with their leader. 

Thor Heyerdahl, [3mEaster Island [0m(New York: Random House, 1989). Heyerdahlโs most recent defense of his hypothesis did little to 
convince skeptics, but his account of how the islanders moved the statues is interesting and the volume is beautifully 
illustrated. 

Paul Bahn and John Flenley, [3mEaster Island, Earth Island [0m(London: Thames & Hudson, 1992). The most recent and best popular account 
of the pro-Polynesian, anti-Heyerdahl position. The bookโs only flaw is that the authors insist on treating Easter Islandโs 
ecological crisis as a metaphor for the earthโs, an approach that makes for admirable environmentalism but potentially dubious 
history. 

Steven Roger Fischer, ed., [3mEaster Island Studies [0m(Oxford: Oxbow, 1993). A useful if specialized collection of essays on the 
islandโs natural history, settlement, archaeology, traditions, language, script, and arts. 

Jo Anne Van Tilburg, [3mEaster Island [0m(Washington, D.C.: Smithsonian Institution Press, 1994). A thorough but somewhat academic 
overview of the islandโs archaeology, ecology, and culture. 

Steven Roger Fischer, [3mRongorongo [0m(Oxford: Clarendon Press, 1997). How Fischer (sort of) cracked the code of the islandโs 
mysterious hieroglyphiclike script.

Wilfred Jewkes and Jerome Landfield, [3mJoan of Arc [0m(New York: Harcourt, Brace, & World, 1964). Includes the relevant sections of the 
original trial and the trial of rehabilitation. 

Jules Michelet, [3mJoan of Arc, [0mtrans. Albert Guerard (Ann Arbor: University of Michigan Press, 1957). The French original, published 
in 1841, transformed Joan into a republican heroine by stressing that her devotion was to the kingdom, not the king. 

Anatole France, [3mThe Life of Joan of Arc, [0mtrans. Winifred Stephens (New York: John Lane, 1908). Joan as the dupe (albeit heroic) of 
priests who heard about her hallucinations and decided to make use of them. 

Vita Sackville-West, [3mSaint joan of Arc [0m(Garden City, N.Y.: Doubleday, Doran, 1938). Both a biography and a meditation on the 
nature of religious belief. 

Regine Pernoud, [3mJoan of Arc, [0mtrans. Edward Hyams (New York: Stein & Day, 1966). Joan, as seen through her own words and those of 
the other witnesses at her trial. 

Maurice David-Darnac, [3mThe True Story of the Maid of Orlรฉans, [0mtrans. Peter de Polnay (London: W. H. Allen, 1969). In this latest 
version of the bastardy theory, Joan proves her royal birth by showing Charles a gold ring engraved with the arms of the House of 
Orlรฉans. She also manages to escape the stake. 

Edward Lucie-Smith, [3mJoan of Arc [0m(London: Allen Lane, 1976). A psychological approach to Joan thatโs sometimes insightful, often 
not. 

Frances Gies, [3mJoan of Arc [0m(New York: Harper & Row, 1981). A straightforward biography, clear and unbiased, though offering no new 
interpretations. 

Marina Warner, [3mJoan of Arc [0m(New York: Alfred A. Knopf, 1981). How Joan fitted into traditions of thought about women, in her own 
time and after. 

Anne Barstow, [3mJoan of Arc [0m(Lewiston, N.Y.: Edwin Mellen Press, 1986). Compares Joan to other late-medieval mystics and heretics. 

Regine Pernoud and Marie-Veronique Clin, [3mJoan of Arc, [0mtrans. and rev. Jeremy du Quesnay Adams (New York: St. Martinโs Press, 
1998). Pernoud is the leading French scholar on Joan, but itโs unclear whether she meant this to be a narrative history or an 
encyclopedia.

to the fall of the Berlin Wall, which deprived the author of access to some articles in English and French journals.

John Cummins, [3mThe Voyage of Christopher Columbus [0m(New York: St. Martinโs Press, 1992). The most recent translation of Columbusโs 
journal is especially interesting because it incorporates the sections recorded by Ferdinand Columbus as well as those preserved 
by Las Casas. 

Ferdinand Columbus, [3mThe Life of The Admiral Christopher Columbus by his Son Ferdinand, [0mtrans. Benjamin Keen (New Brunswick, N.J.: 
Rutgers University Press, 1959). Ferdinand, known as a somewhat bookish man, had a tendency to overemphasize the scholarly basis 
of his fatherโs Enterprise of the Indies, sometimes at the expense of his fatherโs more businesslike qualities. But heโs still a 
remarkable biographer as well as son. The book was first published in 1571, thirty-one years after Ferdinandโs death. 

Henry Vignaud, [3mThe Columbian Tradition on the Discovery of America [0m(Oxford: Clarenden Press, 1920). Columbus was a fraud, and 
historians were his dupes. 

Samuel Eliot Morison, [3mAdmiral. of the Ocean Sea [0m(Boston: Little, Brown, 1942). Still the definitive biography. 

Samuel Eliot Morison, [3mThe Great Explorers [0m(New York: Oxford University Press, 1978). The section on Columbus includes a summary of 
the traditional view on Columbusโs intended destination. While youโre at it, read the rest of the book; there was no better 
historian of the sea than Morison. 

Kirkpatrick Sale, [3mThe Conquest of Paradise [0m(New York: Alfred A. Knopf, 1990). Sale offers one of the latest (and best) 
presentations of the Vignaud position as part of a more general attack on Columbus, in which he blames the explorer for just about 
everything that went wrong with America, from enslaving blacks and Indians to destroying the environment. Not always convincing, 
but always lively and provocative. 

John Noble Wilford, [3mThe Mysterious History of Columbus [0m(New York: Alfred A. Knopf, 1991). An absorbing survey of the ways 
historians from Columbusโs time on have mythologized, debunked, and otherwise interpreted the man and his journeys. 

Valerie I. J. Flint, [3mThe Imaginative Landscape of Christopher Columbus [0m(Princeton, N. J.: Princeton University Press, 1992). A 
fascinating, though somewhat academic, interpretation of the medieval sources of Columbusโs view of the world; here is, Flint 
writes, โnot the New World Columbus found, but the Old World which he carried with him in his head.โ 

William D. Phillips Jr. and Carla Rahn Phillips, [3mThe Worlds of Christopher Columbus [0m(Cambridge, Eng.: Cambridge University Press, 
1992). A balanced history of the explorerโs life and times, especially strong on his time in Spain. 

Miles H. Davidson, [3mColumbus Then and Now [0m(Norman: University of Oklahoma Press, 1997). A provocative but poorly organized critique 
of Columbus biographies.

James Emerson Phillips, [3mImages of a Queen [0m(Berkeley: University of California Press, 1965). How her contemporaries turned Mary

Jean Plaidy, [3mMary Queen of Scots [0m(New York: G. P. Putnamโs Sons, 1975). Not surprisingly, since Plaidy is also a best-selling

let her heart prevail over her head. 

Jenny Wormald, [3mMary Queen of Scots [0m(London: George Philip, 1988). A portrait of Mary as abject failure, so devoid of political

Delia Bacon, [3mThe Philosophy of Shakspereโs Plays Unfolded [0m(London: Groombridge & Sons, 1857). Nathaniel Hawthorne, who wrote the 
preface to Baconโs book, was apparently moved by her sincerity but disheartened by her increasingly obsessive need to haunt the 
graveyards where she was convinced proof of Baconโs authorship would be found. His preface concludes that โit is for the public to 
say whether my countrywoman has proved her theory.โ Later Hawthorne came to regret even that tepid support, stating that โthis 
shall be the last of my benevolent follies, and I never will be kind to anybody again.โ 

Ignatius Donnelly, [3mThe Great Cryptogram: Francis Baconโs Cipher in the So-Called Shakespeare Plays [0m(Chicago: R. S. Peale, 1888). 
According to Donnelly, Bacon wrote not just Shakespeareโs plays but also (in his spare time) Spenserโs [3mThe Faerie Queene [0mand 
Sidneyโs [3mArcadia [0m. 

Mark Twain, [3mIs Shakespeare Dead? [0m(New York: Harper & Brothers, 1909). Twain was actually as concerned with the general question of 
literary immortality (including his own) as he was with Shakespeareโs. But heโs always delighted to insult the experts, whom he 
calls โthese Stratfordolators, these Shakesperiods, these tugs, these bangalores, these troglodytes, these herumfordites, these 
blatherskites, these buccaneers, these bandoleers โฆโ 

Edwin Durning-Lawrence, [3mBacon Is Shakespeare [0m(New York: John McBride Company, 1910). Say what you will about Durning-Lawrence, he 
was a good loser. So confident was he that heโd solved the [3mhonorificabilitudinatibus [0mproblem that he offered a hundred guineas to 
anyone who came up with another anagram with the same qualities. A Mr. Beevor proposed Abi Inivit F. Bacon Histrio Ludit, which 
translated as โBe off, F. Bacon, The actor has entered and is playing.โ Durning-Lawrence paid him the money. 

J. Thomas Looney, [3mโShakespeareโ Identified [0m(New York: Frederick A. Stokes Company, 1920). The Oxfordian Looney should not be 
confused with George Battey the Baconian. 

Calvin Hoffman, [3mThe Murder of the Man Who Was Shakespeare [0m(New York: Julian Messner, 1955). The case for Marlowe, which starts off 
with the great advantage that he was a much more important writer than de Vere. But, like the Oxfordians, the Marlovians have to 
get around the basic problem of their heroโs death, in this instance a much-publicized murder in 1593. Hoffmanโs answer is that 
Marlowe faked his own death to avoid being prosecuted for heresy. 

Frank Wadsworth, [3mThe Poacher from Stratford [0m(Berkeley: University of California Press, 1958). A fair and succinct summary of the 
rival claims up to that time. 

James McManaway, [3mThe Authorship of Shakespeare [0m(Amherst, Mass.: Folger Shakespeare Library, 1962). The establishment position, as 
issued by the establishment. 

S. Schoenbaum, [3mShakespeareโs Lives [0m(Oxford: Clarendon Press, 1991). A scholarly yet highly readable survey of Shakespeareโs 
biographers. 

Charlton Ogburn, [3mThe Mysterious William Shakespeare [0m(McLean, Va.: EPM Publications, 1992). Ogburn, the leading Oxfordian, 
inherited the mantle from his parents, both of whom also wrote books on the subject. Ogburn has been more successful than any of 
his predecessors in forcing the academic establishment to pay attention, and his book even earned a respectful response in the 
mainstream journal [3mShakespeare Quarterly [0m. 

Irvin Leigh Matus, [3mShakespeare, in Fact [0m(New York: Continuum, 1994). A systematic rebuttal of the anti-Stratfordian arguments. 

John Michell, [3mWho Wrote Shakespeare? [0m(London: Thames & Hudson, 1996). The most recent and most compelling case that Shakespeareโs 
works were a group effort involving Bacon, de Vere, Marlowe, and Shakespeare himself. 

Joseph Sobran, [3mAlias Shakespeare [0m(New York: The Free Press, 1997). The latest and one of the best of the pro-Oxford books. 

Mark Anderson, โThe Countenance Shakes Spears,โ [3mHarperโs, [0mApril 1999. A description of the work in progress of Roger Stritmatter, 
an Oxfordian who has found that passages underlined in a Bible that once belonged to de Vere show up in various forms in 
Shakespeareโs plays. Traditionalists have responded that the Oxfordians are, once again, being misleadingly selective.

Sigmund Freud, [3mThe Standard Edition of the Complete Psychological Works of Sigmund Freud, [0mtrans. under the general editorship of 
James Strachey in collaboration with Anna Freud (London: Hogarth Press, 1953โ1974). Of these twentyfour volumes, the most relevant 
are volume 7 ( [3mThree Essays on the Theory of Sexuality [0m, published in 1905 and including the first public retraction of the 
seduction theory) and volume 20 ( [3mAn Autobiographical Study [0m, published in 1925 and with a subtly different recollection). 

Jeffrey Masson, ed., [3mThe Complete Letters of Sigmund Freud to Wilhelm Fliess, [0mtrans. from the German by Masson (Cambridge, Mass.: 
Belknap Press, 1985). The unexpurgated version and, regardless of whether you find Massonโs interpretation persuasive, a revealing 
look at Freudโs thoughts in the making. 

Ernest Jones, [3mThe Life and Work of Sigmund Freud [0m(New York: Basic Books, 1953โ1957), three volumes. Jones, himself an important 
figure in the history of psychoanalysis, wrote what remains the most comprehensive biography. 

Jeffrey Masson, [3mThe Assault on Truth [0m(New York: Farrar, Straus, & Giroux, 1984). Some sense of the Freudiansโ reception to Masson 
can be gauged from Janet Malcolmโs description of him as โa veritable Iago, papered over with charm yet filled with motiveless 
malignity.โ 

Peter Gay, [3mFreud [0m(New York: W. W. Norton, 1988). An authoritative biography that, though it fails to directly address most of the 
recent Freud critics, reaffirms its subjectโs intellectual importance and integrity. 

Paul Robinson, [3mFreud and His Critics [0m(Berkeley, Calif.: University of California Press, 1993). A vigorous defense of Freud. 

Frederick Crews, [3mThe Memory Wars [0m(New York: A [3mNew York Review [0mBook, 1995). Includes not just Crewsโs lively essays but also many 
of the equally interesting responses that also appeared in the [3mNew York Review of Books [0m. 

Frederick Crews, ed., [3mUnauthorized Freud [0m(New York: Viking, 1998). A collection of essays by many of the leading anti-Freudians, 
including the literary critics Mikkel Borch-Jacobsen and Stanley Fish, the philosophers Frank Cioffi and Adolf Grunbaum, the 
psychoanalyst Rosemarie Sand, the biologist Frank Sulloway, the psychologist Malcolm Macmillan, and the mathematician Allen 
Esterson. The range of contributors shows from how many different sides Freud is under attack.

[1mููุฒูุฏ ูู ุงูุจุญุซ [0m

Tom Kuntz, ed., [3mThe Titanic Disaster Hearings [0m(New York: Pocket Books, 1998). Transcripts of the 1912 Senate hearings. 

Walter Lord, [3mA Night to Remember [0m(New York: Holt, Rinehart, & Winston, 1955). A real page-turner about the disaster by a popular 
historian who was no relation and in fact one of the harshest critics of Captain Lord. 

Leslie Harrison, [3mA Titanic Myth [0m(London: William Kimber, 1986). The case for Captain Lord. 

Walter Lord, [3mThe Night Lives On [0m(New York: William Morrow, 1986). Lord revisits the Sinking, in the light of new evidence and 
theories that emerged after [3mA Night to Remember [0m. 

Robert Ballard, [3mThe Discovery of the Titanic [0m(New York: Warner Books, 1987). A firsthand account of the twelve-year search for the 
sunken liner, with stunning and eerie photos by the divers. 

Michael Davie, [3mTitanic: The Death and Life of a Legend [0m(New York: Alfred A. Knopf, 1987). A journalistโs thorough investigation 
into the many things that went wrong, from the shortage of lifeboats to the lack of binoculars for the lookouts. 

Don Lynch, [3mTitanic [0m(New York: Hyperion, 1992). Illustrated with haunting paintings by Ken Marshall as well as hundreds of 
photographs that evoke the shipโs Edwardian splendor. 

Leslie Reade, [3mThe Ship That Stood Still [0m(New York: W. W. Norton, 1993). The case against Captain Lord. 

Robin Gardiner and Dan van der Vat, [3mThe Riddle of the Titanic [0m(London: Weidenfeld & Nicolson, 1995). The central mystery of the [3m
Titanic [0msaga, according to Gardiner and van der Vat, is why Captain Smith, who knew there was ice ahead, continued at high speed. 
The book pursues a conspiracy theory that the White Star Line hoped to sink the ship (which was actually not the [3mTitanic [0mbut her 
sister and near twin, the [3mOlympic [0m) as part of an insurance scam. After tantalizing readers with the evidence supporting the 
theory, Gardiner and van der Vat ultimately (and reasonably) reject it as highly improbable. 

Daniel Butler, [3mโUnsinkableโ [0m(Mechanicsburg, Pa.: Stackpole Books, 1998). A straightforward retelling of the [3mTitanic [0mโs story that 
strips away many myths, not just about,the [3mCalifornian [0mbut also about how and why the ship sank, and how various individuals and 
classes behaved that night.

Mark Steinberg and Vladimir Khrustalev, [3mThe Fall of the Romanovs [0m(New Haven, Conn.: Yale University Press, 1995). A useful 
collection of documents including letters between Nicholas and Alexandra, parts of their diaries, minutes of government meetings, 
and other official papers. 

John F. OโConor, [3mThe Sokolov Investigation [0m(New York: Robert Speller & Sons, 1971). Includes translations of sections of Sokolovโs 
report, along with a harshly critical commentary. 

Anthony Summers and Tom Mangold, [3mThe File on the Tsar [0m(New York: Harper & Row, 1976). An impressive investigation of the 
investigator, even though the discovery of the Romanov bones ultimately disproved the Summers-Mangold theory. 

Peter Kurth, [3mAnastasia [0m(Boston: Little, Brown, 1983). An entertaining albeit overly credulous investigation of Anna Anderson. 

Edvard Radzinsky, [3mThe Last Tsar, [0mtrans. from the Russian by Marian Schwartz (New York: Doubleday, 1992). Radzinsky is a prominent 
Russian playwright, and thatโs both the bookโs strength and its weakness. As literature, itโs engrossing and evocative; as 
history, itโs frustratingly vague and undocumented. 

Marc Ferro, [3mNicholas II, [0mtrans. from the French by Brian Pearce (New York: Oxford University Press, 1993). A solid though 
uninspired biography thatโs undermined by Ferroโs belief that some family members survived. 

Robert K. Massie, [3mThe Romanovs [0m(New York: Random House, 1995). A thrilling historic and scientific detective story thatโs 
especially good at describing the DNA evidence and the rivalries among the Russian, British, and American scientists. 

Peter Kurth, [3mTsar [0m(Boston: Little, Brown, 1995). A magnificently illustrated portrait of the lost world of Nicholas and Alexandra; 
even a Marxist historian couldnโt help but be moved by the snapshots of the family before and during their imprisonment. 

Orlando Figes, [3mA Peopleโs Tragedy [0m(New York: Viking, 1996). A comprehensive history of the Russian Revolution, from the end of the 
nineteenth century to the death of Lenin. Figes is scholarly and fair, yet he captures the passions of the period.

Otto Strasser, [3mHitler and I, [0mtrans. from the German by Gwenda David and Eric Mosbacher (Boston: Houghton Mifilin, 1940). Part 
history, part self-justificationโa combination thatโs undeniably titillating but by no means trustworthy. 

Konrad Heiden, [3mDer Fรผhrer, [0mtrans. from the German by Ralph Manheim (New York: Lexington Press, 1944). Full of details that made it 
an important source for later historians. 

Ernst Hanfstaengl, [3mHitler [0m(London: Eyre & Spottiswoode, 1957). Like Strasserโs book, an ex-Naziโs apologia/memoir. 

Walter Langer, [3mThe Mind of Adolf Hitler [0m(New York: Basic Books, 1972). The OSS report from 1943โmaking this, in a way, the 
official u.s. government position on Adolf Hitler. 

Joachim Fest, [3mHitler, [0mtrans. from the German by Richard and Clara Winston (New York: Harcourt Brace Jovanovich, 1973). The best 
German biography of Hitler. 

John Toland, [3mAdolf Hitler [0m(Garden City, N.Y.: Doubleday, 1976). An anecdotal and very readable biography. As for Raubal, Tolandโs 
theory was that it was her jealousy, not Hitlerโs, that led to her suicide. He came to this conclusion after interviewing a couple 
of the surviving household staff members. They told him that just prior to her death, Raubal had been very upset because sheโd 
found a letter from Eva Braun to Hitler. 

Alan Bullock, [3mHitler and Stalin [0m(New York: HarperCollins, 1991). Bullockโs 1952 biography of Hitler remains a classic, even though 
this more recent dual biography incorporates new research. 

Ronald Hayman, [3mHitler and Geli [0m(London: Bloomsbury, 1997). Hayman is the latest to argue that Hitler shot Raubal. 

Ian Kershaw, [3mHitler [0m(London: Allen Lane, 1998). The latest and arguably the best biography of Hitler to date. 

Ron Rosenbaum, [3mExplaining Hitler [0m(New York: Random House, 1998). An entirely fascinating and often brilliant mix of intellectual 
history and piercing interviews. Rosenbaum reveals the underlying motivations of historians, and in doing so makes an important 
contribution to our understanding of Hitler. This chapter is heavily indebted to Rosenbaum.

Winston Churchill, [3mThe Grand Alliance [0m(Boston: Houghton Mifflin, 1950). Volume 3 of Churchillโs magnificent history of World War 
II, full of not just the authorโs recollections but also his directives, telegrams, and other documents that illuminate the 
British governmentโs pursuit of the war. 

Ilse Hess, [3mPrisoner of Peace [0m(London: Britons Publishing Co., 1954). Hessโs letters to his wife and son from England, Nuremberg, 
and Spandau Prison. 

James Douglas-Hamilton, [3mMotive for a Mission [0m(London: Macmillan, 1971). As the son of the Duke of Hamilton, Douglas-Hamilton grew 
up immersed in the Hess mystery. Surprisingly, though, his book is more about Haushofer than either Hamilton or Hess. Haushofer 
emerges as a fascinating and tragic figure: with ties to both the Nazis and the resistance, he ended up despising himself and 
welcoming his death at the hands of the Gestapo. 

W. Hugh Thomas, [3mHess: A Tale of Two Murders [0m(London: Hodder & Stoughton, 1988). Thomas, a British surgeon who examined Spandauโs 
inmate, discovered to his amazement that he had none of the scars that should have remained from Hessโs World War I injuries. 
Thomas concluded that the man who died in prison in 1987 couldnโt have been Hess. His theory was that Himmler shot down the real 
Hess over the North Sea, then sent his carefully schooled double to England. Thomasโs 1979 book [3mThe Murder of Rudolf Hess [0mmakes 
many of the same arguments. A 1989 Scotland Yard report reasonably concluded otherwise. 

John Costello, [3mTen Days to Destiny [0m(New York: William Morrow, 1991). Though Costello didnโt succeed in overturning the traditional 
view of the Hess mission, his book makes a convincing case that at least some British leaders were not averse to a deal with 
Hitler. 

Peter Padfield, [3mHess [0m(London: Weidenfeld & Nicolson, 1991). Hess as the pawn of [3mboth [0mHitler and Churchill. 

Louis Kilzer, [3mChurchillโs Deception [0m(New York: Simon & Schuster, 1994). Goes even farther than Costello in arguing that some 
inside the British government were behind the German invasion of Russia. Though provocative, Kilzer (like Stalin) is too eager to 
explain everything by conspiracies. Sometimes (though, of course, not always) the official story also turns out to be the real 
story.

ยซูููุงุฐุง ุฅุฐู ุฃุฑุบุจ ูู ุฅูุณุงุฏูุงุยป 

[1mููุฒูุฏ ูู ุงูุจุญุซ [0m

Mikhail Gorbachev, [3mThe August Coup [0m(New York: HarperCollins, 1991). Inevitably, since it appeared just months after the coup, much 
of the book consists of reworked previous statements and speeches, but these still make for a useful document. 

David Remnick, [3mLeninโs Tomb [0m(New York: Random House, 1993). Intimate and revealing vignettes from the last days of the Soviet 
Empire. 

John Dunlop, [3mThe Rise of Russia and the Fall of the Soviet Empire [0m(Princeton, N.J.: Princeton University Press, 1993). Dunlop 
makes a strong case that the plotters had reason to believe Gorbachev could be convinced to side with them. Somewhat academic in 
its style but still compelling. 

Victoria Bonnell, Ann Cooper, and Gregory Freidin, eds., [3mRussia at the Barricades [0m(Armonk, N.Y.: M. E. Sharpe, 1994). A collection 
of documents, interviews, and eyewitness accounts of the coup. 

Valery Boldin, [3mTen Years That Shook the World [0m(New York: Basic Books, 1994). A venomous and vindictive portrait of Gorbachev, 
written by his chief of staff while he awaited trial for his part in the coup. 

Boris Yeltsin, [3mThe Struggle for Russia [0m(New York: Times Books, 1994). Disorganized and sometimes disingenuous, but entertaining 
and informative. Yeltsin has little good to say about his archrival, though he stops short of accusing Gorbachev of planning or 
approving the coup. Instead Yeltsin concludes, somewhat ambiguously, that Gorbachev was its โchief catalyst.โ As for the 
revisionist attacks on Yeltsinโs own role in the coup, he admits he had a telephone conversation with Kryuchkov during which the 
KGB chief told him the emergency committee would not use military force. But Yeltsin adds that there were good reasons not to 
believe him. 

Jack Matlock, [3mAutopsy on an Empire [0m(New York: Random House, 1995). Matlock, who was the U.S. ambassador to Moscow under Reagan and 
Bush, provides a firsthand account of the Soviet collapse. Unlike the works of most diplomats, Matlockโs is not just a valuable 
historical document but also a valuable work of history. 

David Pryce-Jones, [3mThe Strange Death of the Soviet Empire [0m(New York: Henry Holt, 1995). An unusual and often interesting mix of 
interviews with former leaders, dissidents, and other Soviet observers. 

Mikhail Gorbachev, [3mMemoirs [0m(New York: Doubleday, 1995). Like its author, the book goes on too long but remains important. 

Archie Brown, [3mThe Gorbachev Factor [0m(Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1996). The best overall defense of Gorbachevโs record as a 
genuine reformerโa better defense, in fact, than Gorbachevโs own memoirs. 

Amy Knight, [3mSpies without Cloaks [0m(Princeton, N.J.: Princeton University Press, 1996). The first chapter is the most recent and 
most thorough case against Gorbachev. The rest of the book argues that the postcoup KGB continues to be a dangerous and 
independent force in Russia. 

David Remnick, [3mResurrection [0m(New York: Random House, 1997). A surprisingly hopeful view of postcoup Russia, especially since most 
of the book chronicles the countryโs descent into chaos, corruption, and crime.
